<div id="review_3311987869" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3311987869" />
    <a title="Paul Haspel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40256937-paul-haspel"><img alt="Paul Haspel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1425160128p2/40256937.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3311987869?book_show_action=true">May 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Haspel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Haspel" href="/user/show/40256937-paul-haspel">Paul Haspel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780394517483" href="/book/show/1360945.Gorky_Park">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40256937-paul-haspel?shelf=cold-war">cold-war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40256937-paul-haspel?shelf=soviet-union">soviet-union</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3311987869" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3895173283408379125">Gorky Park – officially, the Gorky Central Park of Culture and Leisure (Центральный парк культуры и отдыха имени Горького)– plays a role in Moscow life similar to that of Central Park in New York City. In both cases, the park offers green space for rest and renewal in the middle of a major city, along with recreational opportunities. Yet while Gorky Park was named for the Soviet writer Maxim Gorky, it is today best known for its associations with an American writer – Martin Cruz Smith, who made </span>
  <span id="freeText3895173283408379125" style="display:none">Gorky Park – officially, the Gorky Central Park of Culture and Leisure (Центральный парк культуры и отдыха имени Горького)– plays a role in Moscow life similar to that of Central Park in New York City. In both cases, the park offers green space for rest and renewal in the middle of a major city, along with recreational opportunities. Yet while Gorky Park was named for the Soviet writer Maxim Gorky, it is today best known for its associations with an American writer – Martin Cruz Smith, who made Gorky Park the thematic epicenter, and the title, of one of the best police-procedural novels ever written.<br /><br />At first glance, there doesn’t seem to be much in Smith’s background that would point ahead toward his writing a series of detective novels set in the Soviet Union and later the Russian Federation. Born in Pennsylvania, Smith is not of Russian or Eastern European heritage; rather, his mother is of Pueblo ancestry and was active in Indigenous Rights movements. Smith’s Native American heritage no doubt influenced the composition of books like <i>Nightwing</i> (1977), a supernatural thriller set on the Hopi Nation, and <i>Stallion Gate</i> (1986), a political suspense novel whose Pueblo protagonist finds himself involved in espionage intrigue in New Mexico at the time of the development of the atomic bomb. <br /><br />But Smith has always had a strong interest in writing fiction that crosses cultural boundaries – an early series of his mystery novels featured a Romani or “gypsy” art dealer turned detective – and <i>Gorky Park</i> certainly follows in that tradition.<br /><br />Published in 1981, in one of the chilliest periods of the Cold War, <i>Gorky Park</i> begins with the discovery of three bodies in the Moscow park that gives the novel its title. What makes this murder different is that the killer sliced off the faces and fingertips of all three victims – a gruesome extra touch that might make it seem, at first, as though the identities of the murder victims could never be discovered.<br /><br />It is a standard trope of the police procedural to begin with the discovery of the victim(s), and then to move on to the introduction of the detective whose job it will be to solve the murder(s). In the case of <i>Gorky Park</i>, the protagonist is Arkady Renko, a chief investigator for the <i>militsiya</i>, the regular Moscow police. Even before the finding of the Gorky Park corpses, Renko has been having plenty of troubles of his own. His life and work are always placed in the shadow of his father, a famously bloodthirsty World War II general who despises his son as “weak.” He has powerful enemies among the KGB. And as if all that weren’t enough, his wife is openly having an affair.<br /><br />What protects Renko is his excellent work as an investigator, his known loyalty to Russia (though not the Soviet Union), his ability to think on his feet, a Stoic approach to life, and an ironic sense of humour. All of these character traits make him an interesting and sympathetic protagonist – a hero who does not want to be one; a true hero in a society full of false ones.<br /><br />Renko, through diligent police work, uncovers evidence that one of the Gorky Park victims was an American – a development that would transfer the case from <i>militsiya</i> to KGB jurisdiction, and move him safely off of a politically explosive and dangerous case. And yet, there is a part of him that wants to stay on, to <i>solve</i> the case, even if he sometimes wonders whether he is overthinking the case, making it more complex than it truly is:<br /><br /><i>Was he a chief investigator or a processor of the dead, an adjunct of the morgue, his paperwork the bureaucratic substitute for last rites? A small point, that, and merely indicative of socialist reality (after all, only Lenin Lives!). More important, career-wise, everyone was right. Unless he became a party apparatchik, he’d gone as far as he ever would. Here and no further. Was it possible – did he have the imagination – to create some elaborate case full of mysterious foreigners, black marketeers and informers, a whole population of fictitious vapors rising off three corpses? All of it a game of the investigator against himself? There was a certain plausibility to that.</i> (p. 102)<br /><br />As he continues with his investigation, Renko meets a range of vividly rendered supporting characters, all of whom seem to be somehow connected to the Gorky Park murders. Those characters include John Osborne, a wealthy and well-connected American businessman who regularly travels to the U.S.S.R. to purchase Barguzin sables for the fur trade, since the Soviet Union has a monopoly on those sables. William Kirwill, a tough New York City police detective, has come to Moscow in search of his missing brother Jimmy, who may be one of the Gorky Park victims. Irina Asanova, a beautiful young Siberian dissident, also seems to have connections to the victims; and from the disdain with which Renko and Irina speak to each other, it is clear that the two are falling deeply in love.<br /><br />As mentioned above, the fact of the murder victims lacking faces and fingertips might seem to make positive identification of the victims impossible. Yet – and this is no doubt one of the factors that influenced novelist Smith in composing this novel – the U.S.S.R. was home to a brilliant real-life scientist, an archaeologist and anthropologist named Mikhail M. Gerasimov, who could reconstruct a human face from the bones and tissue left behind after the flesh decayed, and had done so in the case of historical figures like Ivan the Terrible. <br /><br />In Smith’s <i>Gorky Park</i>, the scientist is one Professor Andreev from the University of Moscow; and while the professor normally (and wisely) stays away from anything that could be considered political, he is intrigued enough in this instance to take on the professional challenge of reconstructing the faces of the Gorky Park victims. Kirwill is singularly dubious regarding this bit of investigative initiative on Renko’s part – “A face from a skull?...Well, this is fascinating, like seeing police procedure in ancient Rome. What’s next, entrails from birds, or do you throw bones?” (p. 204) – but this line of inquiry does produce important results for Renko’s investigation.<br /><br />Indeed, Renko’s diligent detective work leads him to the killer – and to a stark scene of confrontation when Renko confronts the killer, just as the killer is about to go into a party through the Kremlin’s Trinity Gate. The killer, untouchable at the moment because of his connections among the Soviet <i>nomenklatura</i>, asks whether Renko is really willing to run the risk of making a politically inadvisable arrest, given the depth of corruption throughout Soviet society: “You can’t be willing to die simply to make an arrest to please Soviet justice. Everyone is bought, from the top to the bottom. The whole country’s bought – bought cheap, cheapest in the world. You don’t care about breaking laws, you’re not that stupid anymore. So what is there to die for?” (p. 306)<br /><br />The killer is right about the endemic corruption of the Soviet Union, as an act of betrayal by a long-time friend and mentor very nearly costs Renko his life, and drops him into the clutches of the KGB into the bargain. Yet even the fanatically politicized agents of the Soviet secret police must acknowledge Renko’s incorruptible honesty; and the dedicated <i>militsiya</i> investigator continues to move closer to final resolution of the Gorky Park case.<br /><br />And resolving the case, in a nice twist, takes Renko to the other side of the Iron Curtain – to New York City – where the prospect of an exchange involving smuggled goods also holds forth the possibility of freedom in the West for Arkady Renko and Irina Asanova. Yet Renko sees that the prospects for a happy resolution of their dangerous situation are remote at best:<br /><br /><i>Then he and Irina might not get away. Perhaps the FBI watched the windows of their room all the time. Arkady had never driven an American car; who knew how it worked? They could get lost. Maps, at least in the Soviet Union, were deliberately inaccurate. Perhaps he and Irina were so plainly Russian that everyone would recognize them as fugitives. Besides, he was an ignorant man in a foreign country.</i> (p. 410)<br /><br />And thus <i>Gorky Park</i> moves toward its suspenseful conclusion, thousands of miles from the park that gave the novel its name.<br /><br />Smith sets forth the people and events of <i>Gorky Park</i> in what I would call a sort of tough-minded prose poetry. He sketches his characters economically and effectively, and weaves an intricate plot with plenty of authentic surprises. While I quite like the stylish 1983 film adaptation by British director Michael Apted (with Helsinki standing in for Moscow, as Western film crews could not film in Russia in those days), the novel is an ever deeper and richer fictive experience – a marvelous way for post-Cold War readers to be, for a time, back in the U.S.S.R.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3895173283408379125" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_129855476" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/129855476" />
    <a title="David Jackmanson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4513870-david-jackmanson"><img alt="David Jackmanson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1330337632p2/4513870.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/129855476?book_show_action=true">Nov 08, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Jackmanson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Jackmanson" href="/user/show/4513870-david-jackmanson">David Jackmanson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer129855476" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17831805665275494709">One of my favourite noir novels ever, a story I keep coming back to. The first time I read this book I thought it was just typical USAian triumphalism over the Soviet Union, but I was wrong. The USA is shown as a place where it's a little easier to breathe, but it's dominated by the rich and powerful just as the Soviet Union is.<br/><br/>Arkady Renko is a prosecutor's investigator for homicide in Moscow in the late 1970s. He is called to a murder scene in Gorky Park, Moscow's favourite place to forget the</span>
  <span id="freeText17831805665275494709" style="display:none">One of my favourite noir novels ever, a story I keep coming back to. The first time I read this book I thought it was just typical USAian triumphalism over the Soviet Union, but I was wrong. The USA is shown as a place where it's a little easier to breathe, but it's dominated by the rich and powerful just as the Soviet Union is.<br /><br />Arkady Renko is a prosecutor's investigator for homicide in Moscow in the late 1970s. He is called to a murder scene in Gorky Park, Moscow's favourite place to forget the world for a little, where three bodies with their faces cut off and their fingertips sheared have lain for several months. He desperately tries to get out of the case, supposing that the victims were killed by the KGB, whose Major Pribluda is an enemy of Renko. But the case sticks to him like a friend who needs a loan, and Renko is forced into intrigue and lies so he can either do his job, or avoid it.<br /><br />Suspicion falls on a visiting USAian businessman, a fur dealer, whose aura of money and power make the drabness of Moscow even duller than it really is. But while this seems the perfect excuse to shunt the case over to the KGB, Renko still is stuck with it.<br /><br />Gorky Park is a book about power, about what people who have it are like and what they get to do to people who don't. The details of everyday life in Moscow are fascinating, and the murder is gruesome enough to excite people who like that sort of thing but these are ultimately side issues. Martin Cruz Smith's book is about what it's like to live in a world where the rich and powerful can get away with almost anything - and he's not just talking about the Soviet Union.<br /><br />NB: For an essay that perfectly explains why I enjoy noir fiction, see <a href="http://www.en.utexas.edu/amlit/amlitprivate/scans/chandlerart.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Raymond Chandler's <i>The Simple Art of Murder</i></a>:<br /><br /><blockquote>The realist in murder writes of a world in which gangsters can rule nations and almost rule cities, in which hotels and apartment houses and celebrated restaurants are owned by men who made their money out of brothels, in which a screen star can be the fingerman for a mob, and the nice man down the hall is a boss of the numbers racket; a world where a judge with a cellar full of bootleg liquor can send a man to jail for having a pint in his pocket, where the mayor of your town may have condoned murder as an instrument of moneymaking, where no man can walk down a dark street in safety because law and order are things we talk about but refrain from practising; a world where you may witness a hold-up in broad daylight and see who did it, but you will fade quickly back into the crowd rather than tell anyone, because the hold-up men may have friends with long guns, or the police may not like your testimony, and in any case the shyster for the defense will be allowed to abuse and vilify you in open court, before a jury of selected morons, without any but the most perfunctory interference from a political judge.<br /><br />It is not a very fragrant world, but it is the world you live in, and certain writers with tough minds and a cool spirit of detachment can make very interesting and even amusing patterns out of it. It is not funny that a man should be killed, but it is sometimes funny that he should be killed for so little, and that his death should be the coin of what we call civilization. All this still is not quite enough.<br /><br />In everything that can be called art there is a quality of redemption. It may be pure tragedy, if it is high tragedy, and it may be pity and irony, and it may be the raucous laughter of the strong man. But down these mean streets a man must go who is not himself mean, who is neither tarnished nor afraid. The detective in this kind of story must be such a man. He is the hero, he is everything. He must be a complete man and a common man and yet an unusual man. He must be, to use a rather weathered phrase, a man of honor, by instinct, by inevitability, without thought of it, and certainly without saying it. He must be the best man in his world and a good enough man for any world.</blockquote><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17831805665275494709" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_770815878" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/770815878" />
    <a title="Eric" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/76688-eric"><img alt="Eric" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1255373698p2/76688.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/770815878?book_show_action=true">Nov 21, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eric" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eric" href="/user/show/76688-eric">Eric</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/76688-eric?shelf=audiobooks">audiobooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/76688-eric?shelf=detective-mystery">detective-mystery</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer770815878" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14762440619719111073">While I didn't always love the experience of reading this novel, I am glad to have read it, if only for the fictional glimpse of Soviet Russia during the Cold War. I didn't enjoy how drawn out the book became after such an intriguing start. But then, I was only expecting a police procedural set in Russia. This novel was much, much more -- a cat and mouse game, a story of fugitives and bandits, a view of Soviet "justice," a story of torture, a social commentary on America by a Russian narrator, a</span>
  <span id="freeText14762440619719111073" style="display:none">While I didn't always love the experience of reading this novel, I am glad to have read it, if only for the fictional glimpse of Soviet Russia during the Cold War. I didn't enjoy how drawn out the book became after such an intriguing start. But then, I was only expecting a police procedural set in Russia. This novel was much, much more -- a cat and mouse game, a story of fugitives and bandits, a view of Soviet "justice," a story of torture, a social commentary on America by a Russian narrator, a political thriller, and a love story. So while certain parts of it dragged, I can't really fault it for all its ambition. Also, this book taught me a colloquial Russian phrase that seems to be appropriate in almost any situation, "fuck your mother." So hooray for this book expanding my worldliness, and fuck your mother.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14762440619719111073" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_741716424" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/741716424" />
    <a title="Cphe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14907696-cphe"><img alt="Cphe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1378006896p2/14907696.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/741716424?book_show_action=true">Oct 15, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cphe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cphe" href="/user/show/14907696-cphe">Cphe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14907696-cphe?shelf=russian-mystery-thriller">russian-mystery-thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14907696-cphe?shelf=reviewed-on-amazon">reviewed-on-amazon</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer741716424" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1694612892747905126">A well written police procedural, thriller that has stood the test of time. Arkady Renko is a charismatic character, patriotic, intelligent, intense, but also flawed and strangely vulnerable. He's a good police officer trying to investigate a horrific crime within the boundaries of a corrupt and rigid system.<br/><br/>It's the setting, the glimpse of Russian life, politics and culture, along with Arkady Renko that make this atmospheric and quite intricate story so compelling. Well worth a look at. </span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_2333227424" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2333227424" />
    <a title="DeAnna Knippling" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3166570-deanna-knippling"><img alt="DeAnna Knippling" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442526565p2/3166570.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2333227424?book_show_action=true">Mar 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="DeAnna Knippling" class="user" itemprop="url" name="DeAnna Knippling" href="/user/show/3166570-deanna-knippling">DeAnna Knippling</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3166570-deanna-knippling?shelf=top-100-crime-novels-cwa">top-100-crime-novels-cwa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3166570-deanna-knippling?shelf=top-100-mystery-novels-mwa">top-100-mystery-novels-mwa</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2333227424" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9030301859841309965">A 1980 Russian investigator is directed to solve a crime. He'd rather dump it on the KGB; it smells political. But no, they're leaving him holding the bag...<br/><br/>The mystery is too complicated. The plot takes too many irrelevant turns. The romance makes no sense. And yet...perfect. Not a typical mystery plot at all; much more like a shamamic journey than anything else. I loved the ending.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1414835347" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1414835347" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16077597-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582900704p2/16077597.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1414835347?book_show_action=true">Oct 12, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/16077597-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19294429-gorky-park">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16077597-matt?shelf=media-ebooks">media-ebooks</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1414835347" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17935994933705755386"><br/>A murder mystery story mostly set in Moscow. Three bodies have been found frozen and faceless under the snow in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3072763.Maxim_Gorky' title='Maxim Gorky' rel='nofollow noopener'>Gorky</a> Park. Arkady Renko of the Moscow militia sets out to investigate. It turns out this case is far more complicated than usual and Renko soon finds himself entangled in a complex web of conspiracy, corruption, espionage, murder and the smuggling of s— [spoiler removed].<br/><br/>I know the <a href='http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0085615' rel='nofollow noopener'>1983 film base on the novel</a> quite well. Therefore I knew who dunnit (and why) prior to reading the book.</span>
  <span id="freeText17935994933705755386" style="display:none"><br>A murder mystery story mostly set in Moscow. Three bodies have been found frozen and faceless under the snow in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3072763.Maxim_Gorky" title="Maxim Gorky" rel="nofollow noopener">Gorky</a> Park. Arkady Renko of the Moscow militia sets out to investigate. It turns out this case is far more complicated than usual and Renko soon finds himself entangled in a complex web of conspiracy, corruption, espionage, murder and the smuggling of s— [spoiler removed].<br><br>I know the <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0085615" rel="nofollow noopener">1983 film base on the novel</a> quite well. Therefore I knew who dunnit (and why) prior to reading the book. Reading was enjoyable anyway because I could concentrate more on the characters and their motivations than on the actual story. There are also quite some differences (minor and major) and a whole new part that is missing from the movie that made the reading worthwhile. Will I read the other books in the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/41080-arkady-renko" rel="nofollow noopener">Arkady Renko series</a>? Yes, probably. He's an intriguing character as far as Moscow police officers go. <br><br>[3½ stars]<br><br><a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-sa/3.0/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img alt="Creative Commons License" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380148217i/680962.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br>This work is licensed under a <a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-sa/3.0/" rel="nofollow noopener">Creative Commons Attribution-NonCommercial-ShareAlike 3.0 Unported License</a>.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('gLl8rJg0k8/tD4I6sHL2O0+sO2fKQ2WxqpPwX9W3Igj8RWBlnDbjUnTBBBOKf1MosA3LE6CLsXde/wmgz0WUgQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('0Bi2FQ4M46Ev/UzsZJ1nSNRYMXIUiPQ8uW3kLnIq2Sqs5KrcCg6TPLYzysVekMJbK/nBBn5AIPpNAR3RaNhvow==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/8258.Martin_Cruz_Smith">About Martin Cruz Smith</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/8258.Martin_Cruz_Smith"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1252590168p3/8258.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/8258.Martin_Cruz_Smith">Martin Cruz Smith
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
903
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10970459516846206534">AKA <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/342961.Simon_Quinn' title='Simon Quinn' rel='nofollow noopener'>Simon Quinn</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/47972.Nick_Carter' title='Nick Carter' rel='nofollow noopener'>Nick Carter</a>.<br/><br/>Martin Cruz Smith (born Martin William Smith), American novelist, received his BA in Creative Writing from the University of Pennsylvania in 1964. He worked as a journalist from 1965 to 1969 before turning his hand to fiction. His first mystery (<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/890267.Gypsy_in_Amber' title='Gypsy in Amber by Martin Cruz Smith' rel='nofollow noopener'>Gypsy in Amber</a> – 1971) features NY gypsy art dealer Roman Grey and was nominated for an Edgar Award. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/151351.Nightwing' title='Nightwing by Martin Cruz Smith' rel='nofollow noopener'>Nightwing</a> was his breakt</span>
  <span id="freeText10970459516846206534" style="display:none">AKA <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/342961.Simon_Quinn" title="Simon Quinn" rel="nofollow noopener">Simon Quinn</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/47972.Nick_Carter" title="Nick Carter" rel="nofollow noopener">Nick Carter</a>.<br /><br />Martin Cruz Smith (born Martin William Smith), American novelist, received his BA in Creative Writing from the University of Pennsylvania in 1964. He worked as a journalist from 1965 to 1969 before turning his hand to fiction. His first mystery (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/890267.Gypsy_in_Amber" title="Gypsy in Amber by Martin Cruz Smith" rel="nofollow noopener">Gypsy in Amber</a> – 1971) features NY gypsy art dealer Roman Grey and was nominated for an Edgar Award. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/151351.Nightwing" title="Nightwing by Martin Cruz Smith" rel="nofollow noopener">Nightwing</a> was his breakthrough novel and was made into a movie.<br /><br />Smith is best known for his series of novels featuring Russian investigator Arkady Renko. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/762806.Gorky_Park__Arkady_Renko___1_" title="Gorky Park (Arkady Renko, #1) by Martin Cruz Smith" rel="nofollow noopener">Gorky Park</a>, published in 1981, was the first of these and was called "thriller of the '80s" by Time Magazine. It became a bestseller and won the Gold Dagger Award from the British Crime Writers' Association. Renko has also appeared in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/778285.Polar_Star__Arkady_Renko___2_" title="Polar Star (Arkady Renko, #2) by Martin Cruz Smith" rel="nofollow noopener">Polar Star</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/647683.Red_Square__Arkady_Renko___3_" title="Red Square (Arkady Renko, #3) by Martin Cruz Smith" rel="nofollow noopener">Red Square</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/151353.Havana_Bay__Arkady_Renko___4_" title="Havana Bay (Arkady Renko, #4) by Martin Cruz Smith" rel="nofollow noopener">Havana Bay</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/151350.Wolves_Eat_Dogs__Arkady_Renko___5_" title="Wolves Eat Dogs (Arkady Renko, #5) by Martin Cruz Smith" rel="nofollow noopener">Wolves Eat Dogs</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/151346.Stalin_s_Ghost__Arkady_Renko___6_" title="Stalin's Ghost (Arkady Renko, #6) by Martin Cruz Smith" rel="nofollow noopener">Stalin's Ghost</a>, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6362651.Three_Stations__Arkady_Renko___7_" title="Three Stations (Arkady Renko, #7) by Martin Cruz Smith" rel="nofollow noopener">Three Stations</a>.<br /><br />In the 1970s, Smith wrote <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/79551-the-inquisitor" rel="nofollow noopener">The Inquisitor Series</a> under the pseudonym <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/342961.Simon_Quinn" title="Simon Quinn" rel="nofollow noopener">Simon Quinn</a> and penned two Slocum adult action westerns as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/24850.Jake_Logan" title="Jake Logan" rel="nofollow noopener">Jake Logan</a>. He also wrote the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/64502-killmaster" rel="nofollow noopener">Nick Carter: Killmaster series</a> under the alias <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/47972.Nick_Carter" title="Nick Carter" rel="nofollow noopener">Nick Carter</a> with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Mike%20Avallone" title="Mike Avallone" rel="nofollow noopener">Mike Avallone</a> and others.<br /><br />Martin Cruz Smith now lives in San Rafael, California with his wife and three children.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10970459516846206534" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

Just what do you investigate?&quot; Arkady asks Ofelia Osorio, a detective in the Policia Nacional de la Revolucion. &quot;Or is it simply open season on Russians in Havana?&quot;The comrades of the Cold War have parted bitterly, and the Russians who used to swarm through Havana\'s streets are now as rare as they are despised, much more so than Americans.Havana is overrun with color, music, and suspicion. The Revolution\'s heroes have outlived idealism. The Com-munist world has shrunk to Cuba. Paradise has become a stop on sex tours. It is a city of empty stores and talking drums, Karl Marx and sharp machetes, where an American radical rides around in Hemingway\'s car to tout island investments and a Wall Street developer on the run from the FBI flies a pirate flag.&quot;A dead Russian, a live Russian,&quot; Ofelia says. &quot;What\'s the difference?&quot; But the dead Russian is followed by the murders of a Cuban boxer and a prostitute. Although none of them is supposed to be investigated, Arkady cannot be stopped. He speaks no Spanish, knows nothing about Cuba, and, as a Russian, is a pariah. However, there is something about this faded, lovely, dangerous city--the rhythms of waves against the seawall, the insinuation of music always in the air, and, finally, Ofelia herself--that plunges Arkady back into life.&quot;What ultimately sets the Renko books apart is the careful writing, and, more important, the knowledge of the human heart that is carried through it, through them, first to In this tale of Arkady Renko, Smith has again fashioned an unforgettable character as cynical as Philip Marlowe, but with the heart of a Chekhovian Everyman. An unwed teenage mother headed to Moscow to seek a new life. A cruel-hearted soldier looking furtively, forcibly, for sex. An infant disappearing without a trace.\n\nSo begins Martin Cruz Smith’s masterful Three Stations, a suspenseful, intricately constructed novel featuring Investigator Arkady Renko. For the last three decades, beginning with the trailblazing Gorky Park, Renko (and Smith) have captivated readers with detective tales set in Russia. Renko is the ironic, brilliantly observant cop who finds solutions to heinous crimes when other lawmen refuse to even acknowledge that crimes have occurred. He uses his biting humor and intuitive leaps to fight not only wrongdoers but the corrupt state apparatus as well.\n\nIn Three Stations, Renko’s skills are put to their most severe test. Though he has been technically suspended from the prosecutor’s office for once again turning up unpleasant truths, he strives to solve a last case: the death of an elegant young woman whose body is found in a construction trailer on the perimeter of Moscow’s main rail hub. It looks like a simple drug overdose to everyone—except to Renko, whose examination of the crime scene turns up some inexplicable clues, most notably an invitation to Russia’s premier charity ball, the billionaires’ Nijinksy Fair. Thus a sordid death becomes interwoven with the lifestyles of Moscow’s rich and famous, many of whom are clinging to their cash in the face of Putin’s crackdown on the very oligarchs who placed him in power.\n\nRenko uncovers a web of death, money, madness and a kidnapping that threatens the woman he is coming to love and the lives of children he is desperate to protect. In Three Stations, Smith produces a complex and haunting vision of an emergent Russia’s secret underclass of street urchins, greedy thugs and a bureaucracy still paralyzed by power and fear.<\/span>\n Elements of Greek, French, Chinese and Italian masterpieces had been thrown into the barbarian wagon and carted to Moscow and the Master Builder Himself, who had piled them one on the other into the cement towers and blazing torches of His rule, monstrous skyscrapers of ominous windows, mysterious crenellations and dizzying towers that led to the clouds, and yet still more rising spires surmounted by ruby stars that at night glowed like His eyes. After His death, His creations were more embarrassment than menace, too big for burial with Him, so they stood, one to each part of town, great brooding, semi-Oriental temples, not exorcised but used.&rdquo;</span>
